                if ("connection" in navigator) {
                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {
                        var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";

                        preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Biotechnology 2017 35:10"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Draft genome, 994 re-sequenced lines and GWAS for yield-traits provide a resource of genetics and genomics tools for pearl millet researchers and breeders. Pearl millet [Cenchrus americanus (L.) Morrone] is a staple food for more than 90 million farmers in arid and semi-arid regions of sub-Saharan Africa, India and South Asia. We report the &#8764;1.79 Gb draft whole genome sequence of reference genotype Tift 23D2B1-P1-P5, which contains an estimated 38,579 genes. We highlight the substantial enrichment for wax biosynthesis genes, which may contribute to heat and drought tolerance in this crop. We resequenced and analyzed 994 pearl millet lines, enabling insights into population structure, genetic diversity and domestication. We use these resequencing data to establish marker trait associations for genomic selection, to define heterotic pools, and to predict hybrid performance. We believe that these resources should empower researchers and breeders to improve this important staple crop."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Pearl millet [<i>Cenchrus americanus</i> (L.) Morrone] is a staple food for more than 90 million farmers in arid and semi-arid regions of sub-Saharan Africa, India and South Asia. We report the <span class="stix">∼</span>1.79 Gb draft whole genome sequence of reference genotype Tift 23D<sub>2</sub>B<sub>1</sub>-P1-P5, which contains an estimated 38,579 genes. We highlight the substantial enrichment for wax biosynthesis genes, which may contribute to heat and drought tolerance in this crop. We resequenced and analyzed 994 pearl millet lines, enabling insights into population structure, genetic diversity and domestication. We use these resequencing data to establish marker trait associations for genomic selection, to define heterotic pools, and to predict hybrid performance. We believe that these resources should empower researchers and breeders to improve this important staple crop.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Global temperatures are expected to increase from 1 to 6 °C by 2100, with serious consequences for agriculture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="National Research Council (NRC). Advancing the science of climate change (The National Academies Press, Washington, DC, 2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1680">1</a></sup>. This means that climate-appropriate measures to ensure food security are a priority, especially as the human population is projected to reach 9.1 billion by 2050<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="FAO. 
                    http://www.fao.org/fileadmin/templates/wsfs/docs/expert_paper/How_to_Feed_the_World_in_2050.pdf
                    
                   (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1684">2</a></sup>. Crops that are adapted to the predicted environmental changes have been proposed as one solution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Beddington, J. et al. Achieving food security in the face of climate change. Final report from the Commission on Sustainable Agriculture and Climate Change. Copenhagen, CGIAR Research Program on Climate Change, Agriculture and Food Security (CCAFS). (available at 
                    http://www.ccafs.cgiar.org/commission
                    
                  ) (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1688">3</a></sup>. Even now, availability and further improvement of crops that can withstand climate change could reduce the hunger of the 805 million undernourished people living mainly in developing countries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="FAO. World hunger falls, but 805 million still chronically undernourished. 
                    http://www.fao.org/news/story/en/item/243839/icode/
                    
                   (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1692">4</a></sup>.</p><p>Pearl millet (<i>Pennisetum glaucum</i> (L.) R. Br., syn. <i>Cenchrus americanus</i> (L.) Morrone), a C4 grass, is a highly cross-pollinated diploid (2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 14) with excellent photosynthetic efficiency and biomass production potential. It is cultivated as a staple food grain and source of straw for fodder and fuel in arid and semi-arid regions of sub-Saharan Africa, India and South Asia. Climate-smart vegetative, reproductive, and physiological features of pearl millet make this crop well-suited to growth in harsh conditions including low soil fertility, high soil pH, high soil Al<sup>3+</sup> saturation, low soil moisture, high temperature, high salinity and limited rainfall. Pearl millet reliably produces grain in regions that have a mean annual precipitation as low as 250 mm. In the same drought conditions maize (<i>Zea mays</i>), rice (<i>Oryza sativa</i>), sorghum (<i>Sorghum bicolor</i>), bread wheat (<i>Triticum aestivum</i>) and durum wheat (<i>Triticum durum</i>) are likely to fail<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Vadez, V., Hash, T., Bidinger, F.R. &amp; Kholova, J. II 1.5 Phenotyping pearl millet for adaptation to drought. Front. Physiol. 3, 386 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1729">5</a></sup>.</p><p>Pearl millet is cultivated on <span class="stix">∼</span>27 million hectares worldwide and is the staple food for more than 90 million farmers living in poverty. Millet grain is highly nutritious, with 8–19% protein, low starch, high fiber (1.2 g/100 g)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Nambiar, V.S., Dhaduk, J.J., Sareen, N., Shahu, T. &amp; Desai, R. Potential functional implications of pearl millet (Pennisetum glaucum) in health and disease. J. Appl. Pharm. Sci. 01, 62–67 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1736">6</a></sup>, and higher micronutrient concentrations (iron and zinc) than rice, wheat, maize and sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Tako, E., Reed, S.M., Budiman, J., Hart, J.J. &amp; Glahn, R.P. Higher iron pearl millet (Pennisetum glaucum L.) provides more absorbable iron that is limited by increased polyphenolic content. Nutr. J. 14, 11 (2015)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1740">7</a></sup>. Importantly, the potential of this crop to tolerate air temperatures &gt;42 °C during the reproductive phase means that it can be cultivated using irrigation in the very hot summers of northwestern India<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Gupta, S.K. et al. Seed set variability under high temperatures during flowering period in pearl millet (Pennisetum glaucum L. (R.) Br.). Field Crops Res. 171, 41–53 (2015)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1744">8</a></sup>.</p><p>Despite the clear importance of pearl millet in agriculture, the production and productivity of this staple crop are very low, with an average grain yield of just 900 kg/ha. This is because pearl millet is mainly grown in dryland conditions, which are marginal production environments, and with minimal use of commercial inputs, such as, adequate irrigation, fertilizers and pesticides. Genetic gains, the rate of increase in yield over a given time period, during 1996–2013 in pearl millet have averaged around 24 kg of grain/ha/year in India, which has the highest millet productivity and production of the main pearl millet growing countries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Yadav, O.P. &amp; Rai, K.N. Genetic improvement of pearl millet in India. Agric. Res. 2, 275–292 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1751">9</a></sup>. Pearl millet is vulnerable to several foliar diseases including downy mildew (caused by <i>Sclerospora graminicola</i>), Pyricularia leaf spot or blast (<i>Pyricularia grisea</i>), and rust (<i>Puccinia substriata</i> var. <i>indica</i>). Indeed, these pathogen infections can result in massive yield losses and reduced fodder quality. A limited range of genomics tools for pearl millet have impeded the ability of researchers and breeders to exploit methods for improvement, until now.</p><p>To accelerate pearl millet crop improvement, we sequenced the whole genome of reference genotype Tift 23D<sub>2</sub>B<sub>1</sub>-P1-P5. We also resequenced 994 pearl millet genotypes, including 963 inbred lines and single plants from each of 31 wild accessions, in order to understand the population structure, genetic diversity and domestication of this staple crop. We carried out a genome-wide association study (GWAS) to predict yield-associated traits in both irrigated and drought conditions. We also used genomic prediction to predict hybrid performance. These applications highlight the utility of our resequencing data set for accelerating breeding and enhancement of genetic gains in pearl millet.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly</h3><p>To assemble the pearl millet genome, we used whole genome shotgun (WGS) and bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) sequencing. Ten small inserts (of <span class="stix">∼</span>170, 250, 500 and 800 bp), and 13 large inserts (of <span class="stix">∼</span>2, 5, 10, 20 and 40 kb) WGS libraries were constructed using Tift 23D<sub>2</sub>B<sub>1</sub>-P1-P5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Liu, C.J. et al. An RFLP-based genetic map of pearl millet (Pennisetum glaucum). Theor. Appl. Genet. 89, 481–487 (1994)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1791">10</a></sup> genotype. These libraries were sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2000 and 520 Gb of sequence data, representing 296× genome coverage, were produced (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 1</a>). Two BAC libraries, with an average insert size of <span class="stix">∼</span>120 kb, were constructed from Tift 23D<sub>2</sub>B<sub>1</sub>-P1-P5 using EcoRI and HindIII. 972 Gb of sequence data were generated from 100,608 BAC clones at <span class="stix">∼</span>80× genome coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). In brief, 1.49 Tb of sequence data, after stringent filtering and correction steps, were assembled into 1.58 Gb of contigs (sequences without gaps or Ns) and 1.82 Gb of scaffolds (contigs joined with estimated gaps filled in).</p><p>Based on k-mer statistics, the pearl millet genome size was estimated to be 1.76 Gb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>), indicating that <span class="stix">∼</span>90% of the genome was assembled. Scaffolds longer than 1 kb totaled 1.79 Gb, with 50% of scaffolds (N50) being longer than 884.95 kb (N50 contig = 18,180 bp) and the largest scaffold spanning 4.82 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 3</a>). To evaluate the assembly, we generated additional whole genome sequence data with 1× coverage on the PacBio platform. More than 90% of these long reads were mapped back to a scaffold with more than 90% similarity and 90% ratio of aligned length (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>).</p><p>Linkage information from three biparental mapping populations, and collinearity with the genome of foxtail millet (<i>Setaria italica</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Bennetzen, J.L. et al. Reference genome sequence of the model plant Setaria. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 555–561 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1827">11</a></sup> were used to assemble genomic scaffolds into pseudomolecules. We assembled 1.56 Gb into seven pseudomolecules (Pg1 to Pg7, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 4</a>). The average GC content of pearl millet (47.9%) is higher than that of foxtail millet (46.1%), sorghum (44.5%), barley (<i>Hordeum vulgare</i>, 44.4%), and rice (43.5%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). We assessed the variability in GC content in 10-kb non-overlapping sliding windows (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>) to show that the observed GC content did not arise from sequencing-based GC bias. The GC content in whole genome coding sequence (CDS; 54.76%) and in 384 expanded gene families (53.14%) was examined as well; it was at a similar proportion to the total genome, providing confidence in this result (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). Analysis of completeness was carried out using the core eukaryotic gene mapping approach (CEGMA), which revealed that &gt;97% of genes were present in the assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Pearl millet genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Pearl millet genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3943/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="671"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Genome features in 1-Mb intervals across the seven pseudomolecules. Units on the circumference are megabase values of pseudomolecules. (1) Repeat density, (2) tandem repeat density, (3) gene density, (4) GC content and (5) SNPs identified by resequencing PMiGAP lines in 1-Mb bins. The genome assembly furnished an average GC content of 47.9% and contained 38,579 gene models with mean coding sequence length of 1,014.71 bp.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3943/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repetitive sequences</h3><p>In total, 1.22 Gb of repeat elements were identified in a 1.58-Gb genome assembly, indicating that 77.2% of the assembled genome is repetitive. In addition, because the repetitive parts of the genome are always the parts that are under-represented in the genome assembly, most of the unassembled DNA (0.18 Gb) is most likely repetitive, too. This is not surprising, because multiple repeats will often collapse into a single repeat in an assembly and also because “repeat masking” is often performed before some assembly steps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Bennetzen, J.L. et al. Reference genome sequence of the model plant Setaria. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 555–561 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1883">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Liu, R. &amp; Bennetzen, J.L. Enchilada redux: how complete is your genome sequence? New Phytol. 179, 249–250 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1886">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Al-Dous, E.K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of the date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521–527 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1889">13</a></sup>. We expect the true percentage of repetitive DNA to be a minimum of 80%. This is similar to the proportion of repetitive DNA found in the 2.3-Gb maize genome (&gt;85%), and considerably more than in 730-Mb sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1893">14</a></sup> (<span class="stix">∼</span>61%), <span class="stix">∼</span>400-Mb foxtail millet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Bennetzen, J.L. et al. Reference genome sequence of the model plant Setaria. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 555–561 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1897">11</a></sup> (<span class="stix">∼</span>46%) or 466-Mb rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yu, J. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science 296, 79–92 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1901">15</a></sup> (<span class="stix">∼</span>42%) genomes. In common with the pattern in many other plant genomes, long-terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons were the most abundant class of repetitive DNA, and comprise &gt;50% of the nuclear genome of pearl millet (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 7</a>). Using RepeatMasker, we found that sequence divergence rates were high (peak at 28%) among long interspersed nuclear elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genes and annotation</h3><p>A total of 69,398 transcriptome assembled contigs (TACs), amounting to 43 Mb in total, were identified using pearl millet transcriptome sequences from two different studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Zeng, Y., Conner, J. &amp; Ozias-Akins, P. Identification of ovule transcripts from the Apospory-Specific Genomic Region (ASGR)-carrier chromosome. BMC Genomics 12, 206 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1920">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Rajaram, V. et al. Pearl millet [Pennisetum glaucum (L.) R. Br.] consensus linkage map constructed using four RIL mapping populations and newly developed EST-SSRs. BMC Genomics 14, 159 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1923">17</a></sup> and a new pearl millet transcriptome assembly generated for this study (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 8</a>). <i>Ab initio</i> homology-based gene prediction were combined with transcript assembly to infer a non-redundant set of 38,579 gene models with an average transcript size of 2,420 bp and an average coding sequence of 1,014 bp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#Tab1">Table 1</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 9</a>). The average lengths of mRNA, CDS, introns and exons in pearl millet were similar to those reported for other cereal genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). Among 458 of the most conserved genes in CEGMA, 437 (95.4%) genes were complete but 8 (1.7%) genes were not found in the genome sequence, 8 (1.7%) genes were not included in the gene set, and 5 (1.1%) genes had more than one copy (possibly fragmented genes). In addition, for 956 genes in benchmarking universal single-copy orthologs (BUSCO) analysis, we annotated 96.7% genes, and 95.4% of these are complete. Gene models of rice and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> have been annotated and carefully validated. We chose to use the gene models of rice, which is more closely related to pearl millet than <i>A. thaliana</i>, to investigate the completeness of pearl millet genes. Of the 4,202 single-copy genes in rice, 90.86% have homologs in pearl millet, and 86% of these pearl millet genes were complete when compared with rice gene models (ratio of pearl millet length/rice length 0.8), reflecting the completeness of single-copy genes. Gene density increased toward the ends of pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>), consistent with findings in all other cereal genomes published to date<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Bennetzen, J.L. et al. Reference genome sequence of the model plant Setaria. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 555–561 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1952">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1955">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yu, J. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science 296, 79–92 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1958">15</a></sup>. Most of the annotated genes coded for proteins with homology to proteins in SwissProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45–48 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1962">18</a></sup> (55.61%) and InterPro (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Quevillon, E. et al. InterProScan: protein domains identifier. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W116–W120 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d28003e1966">19</a>) (65.53%). Functions were assigned to 27,893 (72.30%) genes, leaving 10,686 (27.70%) genes unannotated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 10</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of genome assembly</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nbt.3943/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Predicted pearl millet proteins were compared to those already annotated in ten plant species (<i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2550">20</a></sup>, Brachypodium (<i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2557">21</a></sup>, banana (<i>Musa acuminata</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="D'Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2564">22</a></sup>, barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Mayer, K.F. et al. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711–716 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2569">23</a></sup>, foxtail millet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Bennetzen, J.L. et al. Reference genome sequence of the model plant Setaria. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 555–561 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2573">11</a></sup>, maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Schnable, P.S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2577">24</a></sup>, rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yu, J. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science 296, 79–92 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2581">15</a></sup>, sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2585">14</a></sup>, soybean (<i>Glycine max</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2593">25</a></sup> and bread wheat)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ling, H.Q. et al. Draft genome of the wheat A-genome progenitor Triticum urartu. Nature 496, 87–90 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2597">26</a></sup> and, as expected according to evolutionary relatedness, the highest number of orthologs were identified in foxtail millet (74.16%) and the lowest number in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (61.88%; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 11</a>). Reciprocal pairwise comparisons of predicted proteins for 38,579 pearl millet gene models with 385,891 gene models from the same ten plant species (as above) identified 17,949 orthologous groups (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 12</a>), of which 5,232 contained only a single pearl millet gene, which is suggestive of simple orthology (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 13</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). In addition to protein-coding genes, we predicted 909 tRNA, 235 rRNA, 183 microRNA (miRNA) and 752 small nuclear RNA (snRNA) genes in our assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 14</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Gene families</h3><p>We identified unique and shared gene families among different species in the grass subfamilies Panicoideae, Pooideae and Ehrhartoideae using OrthoMCL (Ortho Markov Cluster Algorithm <a href="http://orthomcl.org/orthomcl/">http://orthomcl.org/orthomcl/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2635">27</a></sup>. Pearl millet and foxtail millet share 15,887 gene families (of those, 14,398 are also found in sorghum) while pearl millet and barley share 13,607 gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). A total of 15,869 gene families are present in at least one species in each of the three subfamilies (i.e., Panicoideae, Ehrhartoideae and Pooideae) analyzed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). 354 gene families were substantially expanded in pearl millet and 1,692 gene families were contracted (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). We compared the average length of the genes for the 384 expanded gene families among all the ten species and used “Quantile” statistics concept to estimate the short CDS. In this concept, Q<sub>1</sub> is “25th percentile”, Q<sub>3</sub> is “75th percentile” and interquartile range (IQR) is estimated as Q<sub>3</sub>–Q<sub>1</sub>. We consider a length shorter than Q<sub>1</sub>–3(IQR) to be an extreme outlier. By using this method, we found that only 24 (6.25%) genes had substantially shorter CDS in pearl millet genes compared to other species. Thus, only a small proportion of the expanded gene families might be misidentified because of possible partial genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Gene conservation and gene family expansion and contraction in pearl millet."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Gene conservation and gene family expansion and contraction in pearl millet.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3943/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="295"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Venn diagrams show the number of genes shared between different grass species and among grass families; pearl millet shares 14,398 genes with sorghum and foxtail millet; 13,027 genes with maize and rice; 11,369 genes with barley and wheat. (<b>b</b>) 384 gene families are substantially expanded and 1,692 gene families are contracted in pearl millet compared with other plant genomes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3943/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Expansion and contraction of gene families between species might also highlight differences in bioinformatics analysis carried out for different genomes. Bias in gene model identification among different studies might render a comparison of expansion or contraction challenging. One potential source of bias is if a gene is split, that is, a complete gene is instead annotated as two separate genes. Based on eukaryotic orthologous gene sequences, we estimate that 2.3% of our genes might have been misannotated in this way (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 6</a>). Although we found that 1,692 families were contracted in pearl millet, contraction is 5.4 times more likely than expansion. One explanation may be that there was a far higher proportion of split genes in the reference genomes of the other species that we use for comparison than in our pearl millet assembly. This would make our number of gene family contractions an overestimate.</p><p>Gene families that seem to be the most greatly expanded are those encoding cutin, suberin, wax biosynthetic genes (<i>P</i> &lt; 10<sup>−6</sup>) and transmembrane transporters of secondary metabolites (ABC transporters, <i>P</i> &lt; 10<sup>−24</sup>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM50">Supplementary Table 15</a>). Triterpenoids are a component of wax, and we also observed a substantial expansion of the gene families associated with terpenoid backbone biosynthesis, and monoterpenoid (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05) and di-terpenoid biosynthesis (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.005). Notably, increased cuticular wax synthesis improves drought tolerance in <i>Arabidopsis</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Seo, P.J. et al. The MYB96 transcription factor regulates cuticular wax biosynthesis under drought conditions in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 23, 1138–1152 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2721">28</a></sup>, while reduced wax production has been associated with drought sensitivity in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Zhu, X. &amp; Xiong, L. Putative megaenzyme DWA1 plays essential roles in drought resistance by regulating stress-induced wax deposition in rice. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 110, 17790–17795 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2725">29</a></sup>. An enriched repertoire of genes for lipid synthesis and export of macromolecules in pearl millet might contribute to its heat and drought tolerance.</p><p>Resistance to pathogens is a crucial contributor to crop yield. The majority of resistance genes in plants contain a nucleotide binding site (NBS). Identification of NBS-containing genes in pearl millet will help to identify putative resistance genes. 378 NBS-encoding genes were manually verified after initial searching, comprising <span class="stix">∼</span>1% of the total gene set, similar to the proportion found in other cereal genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 16</a>). NBS-leucine rich repeats (NBS-LRR) genes made up <span class="stix">∼</span>43% of the NBS-genes, with NBS-only genes comprising <span class="stix">∼</span>41%. Of the 378 NBS-encoding genes, 360 were mapped to one of the seven pseudomolecules, with significantly (Chi-squared test <i>P</i>-value &lt; 10<sup>−10</sup>) biased distribution among the pseudomolecules; <span class="stix">∼</span>26.2% and <span class="stix">∼</span>25.7% were located on Pg4 and Pg1, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 17</a>). These are also the same two pseudomolecules to which a downy mildew resistance quantitative trait locus (QTL) was mapped<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Hash, C.T. &amp; Witcombe, J.R. Pearl millet molecular marker research. Internatl. Sorghum Millets Newslett. 42, 8–15 (2001)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2743">30</a></sup>. We observed large tandem arrays of NBS genes near the telomere region of Pg1 (two 4-gene groups, four 5-gene groups and one 6-gene groups) followed by Pg4 (three 2-gene groups and two 4-gene groups) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 18</a>), consistent with a biased distribution of these loci and suggesting that tandem duplication may be an important source of local gene amplification.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Population structure, diversity and domestication</h3><p>To better elucidate population structure, assess genetic diversity and understand pearl millet domestication, we resequenced 994 lines. The lines resequenced comprised 260 inbred male sterility maintainer (B-) and 320 male fertility restorer (R-) lines, 345 Pearl Millet Inbred Germplasm Association Panel (PMiGAP) lines (including cultivated germplasm from Africa and Asia, elite improved open-pollinated cultivars, hybrid parental inbreds and inbred mapping population parents)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Sehgal, D. et al. Exploring potential of pearl millet germplasm association panel for association mapping of drought tolerance traits. PLoS One 10, e0122165 (2015)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2763">31</a></sup>, 38 inbred parents of mapping populations and 31 wild accessions. We generated a total of 1.16 Tb whole-genome resequencing (WGRS) data with 1.68× coverage (<span class="stix">∼</span>3.05 Gb per line) on PMiGAP lines and a total of 116 Gb WGRS data with 1.86× coverage (<span class="stix">∼</span>3.38 Gb per line) on parental lines of mapping populations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM51">Supplementary Tables 19</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM52">20</a>). In addition, for PMiGAP lines, 78.9 Gb of data at an average coverage of 0.12× was generated using genotyping by sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Elshire, R.J. et al. A robust, simple genotyping-by-sequencing (GBS) approach for high diversity species. PLoS One 6, e19379 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2773">32</a></sup>, while for B- and R-lines, 614.45 Gb of data at 0.59× coverage with an average of 1.06 Gb per sample was generated using RAD sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Miller, M.R., Dunham, J.P., Amores, A., Cresko, W.A. &amp; Johnson, E.A. Rapid and cost-effective polymorphism identification and genotyping using restriction site associated DNA (RAD) markers. Genome Res. 17, 240–248 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2777">33</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM53">Supplementary Table 21</a>). Single plants from each of 31 wild accessions sampling the Sahel from Senegal to Sudan were resequenced at an average 2× coverage using WGRS approach (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 22</a>).</p><p>We identified 88,256 simple sequence repeat (SSR) motifs using the <i>MI</i>cro<i>SA</i>tellite program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Thiel, T., Michalek, W., Varshney, R.K. &amp; Graner, A. Exploiting EST databases for the development and characterization of gene-derived SSR-markers in barley (Hordeum vulgare L.). Theor. Appl. Genet. 106, 411–422 (2003)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2797">34</a></sup> in the pearl millet genome sequence and designed primers for 74,891 SSR-containing sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM54">Supplementary Tables 23</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">24</a>), which can be used by the pearl millet community for genetics and breeding applications. Based on resequencing data, we identified 29,542,173 single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) in PMiGAP lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM55">Supplementary Table 25</a> and details for parents of mapping populations and hybrid parental lines <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM56">Supplementary Tables 26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM57">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">28</a>), 3,844,446 insertions and deletions shorter than 50 bp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM58">Supplementary Tables 29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM59">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM60">31</a>), and 423,118 genome-wide structural variations larger than 50 bp such as deletions, duplications and insertions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 32</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Figs. 12–15</a>). We conducted a principal component analysis (PCA) and constructed a neighbor-joining tree based on 450,000 high-quality SNPs. The PCA analysis and phylogenetic tree showed four main clusters, three that contained wild accessions and one that grouped together the cultivated germplasm (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#Fig3">Fig. 3a,b</a>). The three wild accession clusters were separated by geographical origin into East, Central and West African clusters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#Fig3">Fig. 3a,b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Domestication and genetic diversity in elite and wild accessions of pearl millet."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Domestication and genetic diversity in elite and wild accessions of pearl millet.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3943/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="545"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Principal component analysis of 376 pearl millet lines (345 PMiGAP lines and 31 wild accessions) using 450,000 high-quality SNPs. Four different groups were identified: cultivated lines (red) and wild lines from east (blue), west (orange) and central Africa (pink). (<b>b</b>) Neighbor joining (NJ) tree based on 450,000 high-quality SNPs. This analysis also identified separate groups of cultivated and wild lines from east, west and central parts of Africa. (<b>c</b>) Morphological differences between wild (i, ii) and cultivated accessions (iii, iv) of pearl millet. Wild accessions have numerous bristled spikes in the inflorescence and low seed density (i), and a plant architecture characterized by numerous basal and aerial branches (ii), with a plant height of around 1 m. Cultivated accessions have exposed seeds and a high seed density per spike (iii), with a few basal branches and no aerial branches (iv).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3943/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The closest of the wild groups to the cultivated samples is from the central part of West Africa (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>), indicating that pearl millet originated in this region, consistent with prior research<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Oumar, I., Mariac, C., Pham, J.L. &amp; Vigouroux, Y. Phylogeny and origin of pearl millet (Pennisetum glaucum [L.] R. Br) as revealed by microsatellite loci. Theor. Appl. Genet. 117, 489–497 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2877">35</a></sup>. The oldest archaeological remains, which date to 4,500 years ago, were found in the north-central Sahel, in accordance with our genetic analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Manning, K., Pelling, R., Higham, T., Schwenniger, J.C. &amp; Fuller, D.Q. 4500-Year old domesticated pearl millet (Pennisetum glaucum) from the Tilemsi Valley, Mali: new insights into an alternative cereal domestication pathway. J. Archaeol. Sci. 38, 312–322 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2881">36</a></sup>. Studies of archaeological remains found that by 3,500 years ago cultivation of pearl millet was widespread in Sahelian Africa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Amblard, S. &amp; Pernès, J. The identification of cultivated pearl millet (Pennisetum) amongst plant impressions on pottery from Oued Chebbi (Dhar Oualata, Mauritania). Afr. Archaeol. Rev. 7, 117–126 (1989)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2885">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Klee, M., Zach, B. &amp; Neumann, K. Four thousand years of plant exploitation in the Chad Basin of northeast Nigeria I: The archaeobotany of Kursakata. Veg. Hist. Archaeobot. 9, 223–237 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2888">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Kahlheber, S., Bostoen, K. &amp; Neumann, K. Early plant cultivation in the central African rain forest. First millennium BC pearl millet from south Cameroon. J. Afr. Archaeol. 7, 253–272 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2891">39</a></sup>. Spread of pearl millet agriculture to Asia, and in particular to India also dates to 3,500 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Fuller, D., Korisettar, R., Venkatasubbaiah, P.C. &amp; Jones, M.K. Early plant domestications in southern India: some preliminary archaeobotanical results. Veg. Hist. Archaeobot. 13, 115–129 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2895">40</a></sup>. Average pairwise nucleotide diversity within populations (θ<sub>π</sub>) and Watterson's estimator of segregating sites (θ<sub><b>ω</b></sub>) both indicated high diversity among wild accessions (average θ<sub>π</sub> = 0.00366 and θ<sub><b>ω</b></sub> = 0.00342) compared with PMiGAP (average θ<sub>π</sub> = 0.00238 and θ<sub><b>ω</b></sub> = 0.00289) on all seven pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 33</a>). In agreement with the PCA analysis and neighbor-joining tree, we observed strong population structure in the wild accessions and weak population structure in PMiGAP lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Figs. 16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">17</a>). The weak cultivated pearl millet structure suggests a homogenous genetic diversity across large geographical scale. This pattern is certainly associated with a rapid spread of pearl millet agriculture in Africa and India without major bottlenecks during diffusion. This pattern is expected for inbreds derived from a highly allogamous species. The strong structuration of wild diversity and the central geographical origin of the cultivated sample suggest strong untapped and unique diversity for breeding from wild populations found in East Africa (Sudan, Chad) and the West (Senegal, Mauritania).</p><p>Domestication in pearl millet, like that observed in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Schnable, P.S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2931">24</a></sup>, was associated with profound modifications of spike morphology and plant architecture (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). We found several genomic regions that showed reduced diversity in the cultivated (but not wild) species that may harbor genes selected for during domestication. Using a negative log ratio of diversity between cultivated (red) and wild (blue) samples, values close to 1 indicate a tenfold decrease in diversity whereas values close to 0 indicate that diversity is maintained in the cultivated samples. We also identified regions with an excess of differentiation based on a fixation index (F<sub>ST</sub>) measure (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>). These analyses provided orthogonal and consistent results and identified 140 genomic regions with values above the 95% threshold for both loss of diversity and differentiation. Using a stringent threshold of 99.5%, and considering only values identified by both statistics, 24 genomic regions had reduced diversity in the cultivated germplasm, of which eight were located on Pg7, six on Pg6 and five on Pg1 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM61">Supplementary Tables 34</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM62">35</a>). Linkage groups 6 and 7 have previously been identified as carrying QTL that explain most phenotypic differences between wild and cultivated pearl millet germplasm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Poncet, V. et al. Genetic control of domestication traits in pearl millet (Pennisetum glaucum L., Poaceae). Theor. Appl. Genet. 100, 147–159 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2950">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Poncet, V. et al. Comparative analysis of QTLs affecting domestication traits between two domesticated x wild pearl millet (Pennisetum glaucum L., Poaceae) crosses. Theor. Appl. Genet. 104, 965–975 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2953">42</a></sup>. Most of the identified regions have negative Tajima's D values (&lt;−2.0), suggesting a signature of positive selection (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM61">Supplementary Table 34</a>). One striking case of diversity loss of more than tenfold was associated with the regulation of an auxin-induced gene PINOID on Pg6. This gene is known as <i>barren inflorescence2</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="McSteen, P. et al. barren inflorescence2 Encodes a co-ortholog of the PINOID serine/threonine kinase and is required for organogenesis during inflorescence and vegetative development in maize. Plant Physiol. 144, 1000–1011 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2962">43</a>) in maize, and variation in this gene has been associated with phenotypic variation of the inflorescence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Pressoir, G. et al. Natural variation in maize architecture is mediated by allelic differences at the PINOID co-ortholog barren inflorescence2. Plant J. 58, 618–628 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2966">44</a></sup>. Our analyses also pinpointed genes encoding protiens that might be associated with morphogenesis (LIM2 and PINOID on Pg6, Myosin 11 on Pg7) or gene regulation (Basic helix–loop–helix, bHLH110 on Pg3, Zinc Finger on Pg6). Validation of the role(s) of each of these genes in domestication will require functional analyses and further phenotype–genotype association analyses using fine-scale QTL approaches.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">GWAS</h3><p>Genome-wide SNP data were used to compute linkage disequilibrium decay (LDD) in all three germplasm sets. We set the <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> threshold as 0.2 and observed rapid LDD of less than 0.5 kb in B- and R- lines (48 bp) as well as in PMiGAP lines (84–444 bp) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>). LDD in pearl millet is on par with that in maize, and we note that both these plants are allogamous<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Chia, J.M. et al. Maize HapMap2 identifies extant variation from a genome in flux. Nat. Genet. 44, 803–807 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d28003e2985">45</a></sup>. Relatively rapid LDD is expected in sets of lines that represent the variation present in a highly allogamous panmictic population. Grain and stover yield, and its component traits, is of crucial importance in pearl millet and has undergone selection during domestication. We carried out GWAS across 288 test-cross progenies of PMiGAP lines for 20 traits, and identified 1,054 strongly significant marker trait associations (MTAs) for 15 traits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 36</a>): grain number per panicle (91 MTAs), grains per square meter (75 MTAs), stover dry matter yield (kg ha<sup>-1</sup>; 5 MTAs), fresh stover yield (t ha<sup>-1</sup>; 38 MTAs), tillers per plants (147 MTAs), panicle diameter (cm; 1 MTAs), panicle harvest index (%; 1 MTAs), panicle length (cm, EL; 3 MTAs), panicle yield (kg/ha; 9 MTAs), panicle number (ha<sup>-1</sup>; 246 MTAs), plant population (ha<sup>-1</sup>; 68 MTAs), grain yield (kg/ha; 11 MTAs), grain harvest index (%; 5 MTAs), plant height (cm; 344) and 1000 grain mass (g; 10 MTAs). The MTAs explained 9–27% of phenotypic variation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM63">Supplementary Table 36</a>). Selected markers were found common across stress and year for important traits such as grain number per panicle on Pg1 and Pg5 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 20</a>). These markers might be relevant for pearl millet breeding.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genomic prediction of hybrid performance</h3><p>We applied our resequencing data to carry out genomic selection to predict grain yield for test crosses. Four scenarios of prediction were investigated, namely the performance of grain yield in each of the three environments (control, early stress and late stress) and across environments. We observe high prediction accuracy, measured as the Pearson correlation coefficient between the predicted and observed values, standardized with the square root of the heritability (<i>h</i> = 0.78), amounting to 0.6 for the performance across environments. Analyses of this kind have been undertaken for grain yield in other crops using genomic selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Riedelsheimer, C. et al. Genomic and metabolic prediction of complex heterotic traits in hybrid maize. Nat. Genet. 44, 217–220 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3018">46</a></sup>. A modelling study recently found that with this level of prediction accuracy, genomic selection could substantially improve selection gain per year<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Longin, C.F., Mi, X. &amp; Würschum, T. Genomic selection in wheat: optimum allocation of test resources and comparison of breeding strategies for line and hybrid breeding. Theor. Appl. Genet. 128, 1297–1306 (2015)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3022">47</a></sup>.</p><p>We also predicted hybrid performance, by using genomic selection strategy that considers additive and dominance effects. The ridge regression best linear unbiased prediction method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Riedelsheimer, C. et al. Genomic and metabolic prediction of complex heterotic traits in hybrid maize. Nat. Genet. 44, 217–220 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3029">46</a></sup> was trained using phenotypic grain yield data from 64 pearl millet hybrids grown in five environments in India in replicated trials during the time period 2004–2013. The grain yield data were analyzed with 302,110 SNPs with missing values below 5% and minor allele frequency above 5% for 580 B- and R- lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). We found 170 promising hybrid combinations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM64">Supplementary Table 37</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). Of these, 11 combinations were already used for producing hybrids that showed better performance (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM65">Supplementary Table 38</a>). However, 159 combinations have never been used in hybrid breeding (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>), and therefore they are good candidates for developing high-yielding hybrids.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Prediction of hybrid performance."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Prediction of hybrid performance.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3943/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="701"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Grain yield of 64 different pearl millet hybrids, produced by crossing 20 male and 23 female lines, was evaluated at five locations (Jamnagar, Anand, SK Nagar, Mahuva, Kothara in India) during 2004–2013. Phenotyping data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM72">Supplementary Data set 1</a>), together with 302,110 high-quality SNP marker data obtained from 580 B and R- lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM57">Supplementary Table 27</a>), were used to predict hybrid performance. Ridge regression-BLUP, which takes additive and dominance effects into account, was used to predict hybrid performance. (<b>a</b>) Prediction accuracy was studied using 500 cross-validation tests. In each cross-validation, 48 hybrids were randomly selected as a training set and the remaining 16 hybrids were used as a test set. (<b>b</b>) Promising hybrid combinations that include parental lines that have not been used in breeding efforts previously were identified for testing and release as better hybrids. (<b>c</b>) Heat map showing putative heterotic groups.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.3943/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We inspected the predicted hybrid performance of all possible 167,910 single-cross combinations by applying hierarchical clustering combined with a heat plot, and examined the potential of this approach to identify promising heterotic groups. The analyses revealed two sets of lines that are predicted to have an average 8% higher hybrid performance when crossed to each other than the total set of 167,910 single-cross combinations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 21</a>). These predicted high-yield hybrids could be used as a nucleus to establish high-yielding heterotic groups for hybrid pearl millet breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Zhao, Y. et al. Genome-based establishment of a high-yielding heterotic pattern for hybrid wheat breeding. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 112, 15624–15629 (2015)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3093">48</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM64">Supplementary Tables 37</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM65">38</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Pearl millet is a staple food for more than 90 million people in Africa and Asia. People living in arid and semi-arid regions, in particular, rely on pearl millet, which can crop in the harsh conditions. We sequenced the genome of pearl millet reference genotype Tift 23D<sub>2</sub>B<sub>1</sub>-P1-P5 (available at <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/assembly/GCA_002174835.1/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/assembly/GCA_002174835.1/</a>). The draft genome assembly presents 90% of the pearl millet genome with N50 of scaffolds as 884.95 kb and 87.2% assembled genome into seven pseudomolecules. The genome assembly of cereal species like pearl millet with high levels of repetitive DNA is always challenging. Therefore, in addition to a WGS approach, BAC-sequence data were used to develop the draft genome assembly and PacBio data were generated to validate the assembly. To achieve chromosome level assembly, one can use new approaches of sequencing such as Bionano Genomics optical mapping and Dovetail Genomics chromosome confirmation capture data in different combinations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Jiao, W.B. et al. Improving and correcting the contiguity of long-read genome assemblies of three plant species using optical mapping and chromosome conformation capture data. Genome Res. 27, 778–786 (2017)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3123">49</a></sup>.</p><p>Our analysis identified 38,579 protein-coding genes, of which 27,893 (72.30%) were annotated. CEGMA and BUSCO analyses together with comparison with gene models of rice have indicated completeness of predicted genes in pearl millet. Expansion of gene families associated with terpenoid backbone biosynthesis and monoterpenoid and diterpenoid biosynthesis in the genome might explain the high level of heat and drought tolerance in pearl millet as compared to other cereals.</p><p>Genome sequence can provide information either about specific genomic regions or specific genes that are associated with agronomically important traits including grain and fodder yield. Pearl millet fodder is the main feedstock for ruminant (and other) livestock, and breeding to improve fodder quality and yield is of crucial importance to both the meat and the dairy industries. In order to identify loci or variants associated with agronomic features, we undertook a large-scale resequencing effort. Resequencing of the PMiGAP set revealed that small structural rearrangements, such as insertions and deletions in the genome have occurred throughout the evolution of pearl millet. This is similar to observations made in maize: a third or more of maize genes seem to be optional. Frequent insertions and deletions pose substantial challenges to resequencing efforts because self-pollinated and small-genome species such as rice are easier to sequence and analyze as compared to cross-pollinated and large-genome species like maize owing to their increased genomic structural variability<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Genome-wide genetic changes during modern breeding of maize. Nat. Genet. 44, 812–815 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3133">50</a></sup>. With an objective to save the cost, but without losing information, 1.68× coverage WGRS data and 0.12× GBS data were generated on PMiGAP lines and 0.59× coverage RAD-sequencing data were generated on B- and R- lines.</p><p>The sequence information from the more genetically diverse PMiGAP inbred panel will be of broader use for genome-wide association mapping and allele mining. All of these sequences are available at <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov//sra/?term=SRP063925">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov//sra/?term=SRP063925</a>. Resequencing data of almost 1,000 pearl millet lines (963 inbreds of cultivated pearl millet and 31 heterozygous wild individuals, available at <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov//sra/?term=SRP063925">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov//sra/?term=SRP063925</a>) provides researchers and breeders with an enormous resource of genome-wide variations including SNPs, indels, SSRs and structural variations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM54">Supplementary Tables 23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM55">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM56">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM57">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM58">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM59">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM60">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">32</a>) for mining alleles of genes with significant MTAs and for developing pearl millet hybrids with increased heterosis.
