Our analysis on resequencing data on PMiGAP lines together with phenotyping data for 20 traits for GWAS and genomic selection suggests that simultaneous improvement of grain and stover yield might be feasible in pearl millet. Indeed, improved grain and stover yield performance of hybrids in India has been noted over the past 50 years, which underlines the potential for further improvements that could be informed by our analyses.</p><p>We also show the use of the genome sequence and resequencing information to make predictions of test-cross hybrid performance. After inspecting predicted hybrid performance of 167,910 single-cross combinations, we identified 159 pair of lines that have not been used so far for hybrid breeding but can exhibit high hybrid performance. This type of analysis has considerable potential for accelerating future rates of selection gain. Our prediction models were also applied to define heterotic pools for pearl millet for South Asia, which could be crucial for increasing the efficiency of hybrid breeding programs in the same region.</p><p>Together the draft genome and resequencing data provide a resource for the research community that should enable a better understanding of trait variation and accelerate the genetic improvement of pearl millet. For instance, we identified 1,054 MTAs for 15 agronomic traits that will be useful for pearl millet breeding. Our findings will also contribute to a better understanding of the genetic basis of the exceptional drought and heat tolerance of pearl millet as we have identified expansion of gene families associated with drought and heat tolerance. A detailed understanding of how well pearl millet crops do in hot, arid and semi-arid regions might enable engineering of not only pearl millet but also other cereal crops like rice, maize and wheat, which are currently able to provide only limited produce in arid or semi-arid regions. This is especially important owing to the pressing need for heat- and drought-tolerant cereal crops in the coming years.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Plant material.</h3><p>The pearl millet genotype Tift23D<sub>2</sub>B<sub>1</sub>-P1-P5 was bred at the Coastal Plain Experiment Station (Tifton, Georgia, USA) by introducing the <i>d2</i> dwarfing gene into the genetic background of elite seed parent maintainer line Tift 23B1, and was chosen to generate a draft genome sequence.</p><p>Three bi-parental mapping populations were used to develop the genetic map for organizing scaffolds into pseudomolecules. These populations were: (i) a small recombinant inbred line (RIL) population developed at ICRISAT, Patancheru, based on the cross ICMB 841-P3 × ICMB 863B-P2 (MAPPOP1); (ii) a RIL population developed at the Coastal Plain Experiment Station, Tifton, Gerogia (USA) based on Tift 99B × Tift 454 (MAPPOP2); and (iii) an F<sub>2</sub> population derived from a wild × domestic cross (MAPPOP3) from Institut de Recherche pour le Developpement (IRD) France. 580 B- and R- lines included 200 B- and 200 R- lines from ICRISAT plus 60 B- and 120 R- lines from 5 organizations from India namely Haryana Agricultural University, Hisar, Haryana; Junagadh Agricultural University, Jamnagar, Gujarat; Mahatma Phule Krishi Vidyapeeth, Dhule, Maharashtra; Sri Karan Narendra Agriculture University, Durgapura, Rajasthan; and JK Agri Genetics Ltd., Hyderabad, Telangana, were resequenced using restriction-site-associated DNA (RAD) sequencing (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM66">Supplementary Table 39</a>). The PMiGAP lines contains 345 lines: 263 landraces/traditional cultivars, 46 breeding lines, 25 advanced/improved cultivars and 11 accessions with unknown biological status and represents germplasm from 27 countries in two continents (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM67">Supplementary Table 40</a>). These 345 accessions were subjected to WGRS. In addition, 38 inbred parents of mapping populations segregating for drought, downy mildew and rust (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM68">Supplementary Table 41</a>) and 31 wild accessions representing seven countries (Mali, Mauritania, Senegal, Sudan, Chad, Mali and Niger) were also resequenced using the WGRS approach (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 42</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Whole genome shotgun sequencing and assembly.</h3><p>We constructed 10 small insert libraries including 4 with 170 bp insert, 2 with 250 bp insert, 2 with 500 bp inserts and 2 with 800 bp insert, and 13 mate-pair libraries including 4 with 2 kb insert, 4 with 5 kb insert, 2 with 10 kb insert, and 2 with 20 kb insert and 1 with 40 kb insert from pearl millet genotype Tift 23D2B1-P1-P5. To make libraries with <span class="stix">∼</span>170 to <span class="stix">∼</span>800 bp inserts, high quality DNA samples were sheared, end-repaired, and 'A' bases were added to the 3′ end of the DNA fragments to facilitate ligation to adapters. Fragments in the appropriate size range were selected after separation on an agarose gel and amplified using PCR. For mate-pair libraries, a biotinylation reaction was performed after fragmentation and end-repair. Then DNA fragments of the required size were selected and circularized. Circular DNAs were sheared into approximately 400-600 bp fragments, and biotinylated fragments were captured for terminal modification and adaptor ligation to construct libraries. Paired end reads were generated for each library on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform.</p><p>For BAC library construction, DNA from pearl millet genotype Tift 23D<sub>2</sub>B<sub>1</sub>-P1-P5 was fragmented using HindIII and EcoRI, and then ligated into vector pCC1BAC. The ligations were transformed into <i>E. coli</i> DH10b host cells. After DNA isolation from BAC clones, Covaris LE220 system was used to shear DNA into <span class="stix">∼</span>500 bp. Agilent Bravo Automated Liquid Handling Platform and an Agilent BenchCel Microplate Handler were used to construct BACs for sequencing. Then 96-microTUBE plates (Covaris) were used as sample vessels for automated batch processing followed by index adaptor ligation and size selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Wu, J. et al. The genome of the pear (Pyrus bretschneideri Rehd.). Genome Res. 23, 396–408 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3247">51</a></sup>. Generally, the sizes of the BAC ranged from 80 -180 kb and fragments for sequencing were about 500 bp. In total 100,608 BAC clones were constructed and HiSeq 2000 was used for sequencing paired end reads of each BAC clone.</p><p>For each library, we filtered the reads that comprised more than 5 percent of “Ns” or polyA structure, and also removed reads that possessed 20 or more bases with quality score less than or equal to 7. Reads with &gt;10 bp aligned to the adaptor sequence (allowing ≤3 bp mismatch) were considered as adaptor contaminants and removed. Additionally, paired-end reads with a total length smaller than the library insert size allowing a window of 30 bp were removed. We also trimmed the reads if the quality of bases at the head or tail of the reads was low.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">k-mer analysis.</h3><p>We performed k-mer analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Liu, B. et al. Estimation of genomic characteristics by analyzing k-mer frequency in de novo genome projects. Preprint at 
                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1308.2012
                    
                   (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3262">52</a></sup> for the estimation of the genome size of pearl millet genotype Tift 23D<sub>2</sub>B<sub>1</sub>-P1-P5. Genome size was estimated by the formula: Genome size = k-mer_num/Peak_depth where k-mer_num was the total number of k-mers and Peak_depth was the expected value of k-mer depth obtained from the distribution curve. The number of k-mers (generally K = 17) was calculated from short fragment size reads with a one bp slide, and then the frequency of each k-mer was determined. A distribution curve of depth versus frequency was plotted, where the <i>x</i>-axis represents the depth and the <i>y</i>-axis represents the proportional frequency at that depth divided by the total frequency of all the depths.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Development and improvement of genome assembly.</h3><p>For WGS assembly, clean reads were assembled by SOAP<i>de novo</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3286">53</a></sup> (Version 2.04) (parameters: pregraph -s assembly.lib -K 63 -R -d 1 -o pm; contig -g pm –R; map -s assembly.lib -g pm -k 45; scaff -g pm). The k-mer frequency follows a Poisson distribution when read length &lt;&lt; genome size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3290">54</a></sup>. Short insert libraries were assembled into contigs. The reads were mapped back onto the contigs to estimate overlap between contigs. Gapcloser<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3294">53</a></sup> (Version1.10, parameter: -a pm.scafSeq.fill -b reads.lib -o pm.scafSeq.fillGap -t 24) within the SOAP<i>de novo</i> package was used to fill gaps in the scaffold with paired end reads. BAC-by-BAC sequencing of 100,608 BAC clones was conducted to improve the quality of the genome assembly. Each sequenced BAC was assembled separately by SOAP<i>de novo</i>. First, sequences shorter than 2,000 bp or having more than 30% unknown bases in BAC clones were discarded. The remaining sequences were then pooled with WGS scaffolds together to extend and collapse redundant sequences.</p><p>For improving WGS-based assembly, BAC- sequence data were included in analysis using Rabbit package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="You, M. et al. A heterozygous moth genome provides insights into herbivory and detoxification. Nat. Genet. 45, 220–225 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3307">55</a></sup>. This package consists of three modules: Relation Finder, Overlapper and Redundancy Remover. In the first step, 40 bp at the end of each sequence was trimmed as they turn to be of lower quality. Then overlapping between sequences were detected by BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Kent, W.J. BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3311">56</a></sup> with minimum overlap length set to be 3,000 bp. In second module for extension, overlapping with identity greater than 90% were merged and sequences were extended. To avoid the duplicates in the final assembly, segmental duplications and divergent haplotypes were identified and filtered based on the Poisson-based k-mer model following methods described in Liu <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Liu, B. et al. Estimation of genomic characteristics by analyzing k-mer frequency in de novo genome projects. Preprint at 
                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1308.2012
                    
                   (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3318">52</a></sup>. To evaluate the assembly of the pearl millet genome, we first calculated the length and N50 distribution for the BAC sequences. The BAC lengths ranged from 80-140k, and their N50s were from 10-40k (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 22</a>). Gaps can occur in the fragmented BAC assemblies since the insert size of the pair end reads is 500 bp. PacBio reads were processed using Blasr (processed with PBJelly pipeline) to evaluate the assembled sequence.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">GBS and SNP calling on mapping populations.</h3><p>GBS libraries were prepared using restriction enzyme <i>Ape</i>KI as described by Elshire <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Elshire, R.J. et al. A robust, simple genotyping-by-sequencing (GBS) approach for high diversity species. PLoS One 6, e19379 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3340">32</a></sup>. The MAPPOP1 and MAPPOP2 populations were sequenced at 384-plex (that is, 384 samples per flowcell lane) on an Illumina HiSeq 2000, while the MAPPOP3 population was sequenced at 96-plex (96 samples per flowcell lane). SNPs were called using the TASSEL-GBS pipeline in TASSEL v4.1.32<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Glaubitz, J.C. et al. TASSEL-GBS: a high capacity genotyping by sequencing analysis pipeline. PLoS One 9, e90346 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3344">57</a></sup>. The TASSEL-GBS pipeline incurs an overhead for each separate pseudomolecule processed, hence we concatenated the thousands of individual scaffolds into <span class="stix">∼</span>20 megascaffolds to ease computation. Reads were processed into clean 64 bp “tags” and mapped against the reference scaffolds with Bowtie 2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357–359 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3347">58</a>). SNPs were called with the DiscoverySNPCallerPlugin in TASSEL, with minimal filters to reduce the number of false positives due to sequencing errors (minor allele frequency ≥ 0.01, minor allele count ≥ 10, genotype calls in at least 10% of samples) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM69">Supplementary Code 1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">RAD sequencing.</h3><p>Genomic DNA of each B- and R- individual was digested with EcoRI. After electrophoresis, DNA fragments of the desired lengths were gel purified. Adaptor ligation and DNA cluster preparation were performed and fragments were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform. Similarly, 29 DNA libraries were constructed for B- and R- lines (580 samples) and sequenced using the RAD-Seq approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Miller, M.R., Dunham, J.P., Amores, A., Cresko, W.A. &amp; Johnson, E.A. Rapid and cost-effective polymorphism identification and genotyping using restriction site associated DNA (RAD) markers. Genome Res. 17, 240–248 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3363">33</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genetic map construction.</h3><p>SNPs called from the GBS data on three populations (MAPPOP1, MAPPOP2 and MAPPOP3) were first filtered for quality based on minor allele frequency, missingness and heterozygosity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM70">Supplementary Code 2</a>). Linkage groups were defined based on hierarchical clustering of SNPs and ordered with MSTMap. For each population, we created three maps: one from stringently filtered SNPs, one from moderately fileted SNPs, and one mapping GBS sequencing tags back to the stringently filtered map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM70">Supplementary Code 2</a>). The framework map generated in the largest RIL population (Tift 99B × Tift 454) formed the basis of an initial colinearity study between pearl millet and foxtail millet, and the resulting comparative knowledge was used to incorporate additional scaffolds for which orthology to the foxtail millet genome had been established using BLASTP (to identify putative orthologous pearl millet and foxtail millet genes at an E-value threshold of 1e-5) and MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3381">59</a></sup> (to identify colinear segments of at least five syntenic genes between pearl millet and foxtail millet) analyses into the framework map. The genetic maps generated for each of the crosses, and the map that we built based on collinearity information between pearl millet and foxtail millet, were merged using ALLMAPS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps. Genome Biol. 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3385">60</a></sup> with the most weight assigned to the synteny map followed by the stringent SNP maps, the moderately filtered SNP maps, and finally the GBS sequencing tags (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM71">Supplementary Code 3</a>). Linkage group numbering was adopted as per an existing consensus map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Rajaram, V. et al. Pearl millet [Pennisetum glaucum (L.) R. Br.] consensus linkage map constructed using four RIL mapping populations and newly developed EST-SSRs. BMC Genomics 14, 159 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3393">17</a></sup> based on mapping SSR sequences to the assembled genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM71">Supplementary Code 3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Repeat annotation, gene prediction and genome annotation.</h3><p>We searched the genome for tandem repeats with Tandem Repeats Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3408">61</a></sup> (Version 4.04) (parameters: 2 7 7 80 10 50 2000 -d -h). Transposable elements (TEs) were identified in the genome by a combination of homology-based and <i>de novo</i> approaches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Draft genome sequence of chickpea (Cicer arietinum) provides a resource for trait improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 240–246 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3415">62</a></sup>. For homology-based predictions, we used the repeat database Repbase16.10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3419">63</a></sup> to identify known repeats in the genome assembly with the program RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Smit, A.F.A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-3.0 1996–2010 
                    http://www.repeatmasker.org
                    
                   (1996)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3423">64</a></sup> (Version 3.3.0) (parameter: -nolow -no_is -norna -parallel 1 -lib RepeatMaskerLib.embl.lib). At the protein level, RepeatProteinMask, a software in the RepeatMasker package, was used to perform RMBlast against the TE protein database (parameter: -noLowSimple -pvalue 0.0001). For <i>de novo</i> prediction, the programs RepeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Smit, A.F.A. &amp; Hubley, R. RepeatModeler Open-1.0 2008–2015 
                    http://www.repeatmasker.org
                    
                   (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3431">65</a></sup> (Version 1.0.5) and LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3435">66</a></sup> (Version 1.0.5) were used on the entire genome to generate a pearl millet repeat database, which was subsequently used as input library with RepeatMasker (Version 3.3.0) to identify TEs.</p><p>For predicting genes, we applied several approaches: (i) Homology-based prediction: Proteins previously annotated in other species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 9</a>) were mapped to the genome using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Kent, W.J. BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3445">56</a></sup> (Version 34) with default parameters. Alignments in which the coverage of the query protein was less than 0.3 were removed. In addition, if there were multiple BLAT hits (BLAT output was set to the five best hits), secondary hits were removed if their aligned length was less than 0.3 of the aligned length of the top BLAT hit to filter paralogs with lower sequence identity. GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3449">67</a></sup> (with parameter -trev -sum -genesf) was used to predict spliced alignments. (ii) <i>De novo</i> gene prediction: AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, W309–W312 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3456">68</a></sup> (Version 2.5.5,–species = maize–uniqueGeneId = true–noInFrameStop = true–gff3 = on–strand = both) and Fgenesh<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Salamov, A.A. &amp; Solovyev, V.V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516–522 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3461">69</a></sup> (Version 1.3) were used to detect gene models in the repeat masked genome. (iii) Prediction based on transcript sequences: The assembled transcriptome sequences were aligned to the genome assembly using BLAT (Version 34) using the parameters identity ≥ 0.98 and coverage ≥ 0.98 to generate spliced alignments. (iv) Integration evidence: Source evidence generated from the three approaches mentioned above were integrated using GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Elsik, C.G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3465">70</a></sup> to produce a consensus gene set.</p><p>To annotate the function of the final gene models, protein sequences were aligned against KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27–30 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3472">71</a></sup> (release 58) and SwissProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45–48 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3476">18</a></sup> (release 20156) with BLASTP (E-value ≤ 1.0e-05) to find the best matches. InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Quevillon, E. et al. InterProScan: protein domains identifier. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W116–W120 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3480">19</a></sup> (Version4.8, performed with profilescan, blastprodom, hmmsmart, hmmpanther, hmmpfam, fprintscan and patternScan analysis) was used to identify motifs and domains in the proteins encoded by the gene models along with gene ontology annotations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat. Genet. 25, 25–29 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3484">72</a></sup>. For ncRNA annotation, tRNA genes in the assembly were identified by tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Lowe, T.M. &amp; Eddy, S.R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3488">73</a></sup> (Version 1.23). rRNA genes were aligned with plant query sequences (rRNA from <i>Arabidopsis</i> and rice species) using BLASTN with an E-value threshold of 1.0e-05. Other non-coding RNAs, such as miRNAs and snRNAs were predicted by homology searches against the Rfam database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Griffiths-Jones, S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, D121–D124 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3496">74</a></sup> using the INFERNAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Nawrocki, E.P., Kolbe, D.L. &amp; Eddy, S.R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335–1337 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3500">75</a></sup> (Version 0.81) software.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">RNA seq data generation and development of transcriptome assembly.</h3><p>The transcriptome sequence data were generated from individuals “9-8” and “3-9” accessions at IRD. Library preparation and sequencing (PE 100 bp) on an Illumina Hi-Seq 2000 platform was performed by Fasteris (Plan-les-Ouates, Switzerland). A total of 81,207,232 and 74,187,066 sequence reads were obtained for “3-9” and “9-8”, respectively. Adaptor sequences were trimmed and reads were processed for <i>de novo</i> assembly using Velvet 1.0.18<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Zerbino, D.R. &amp; Birney, E. Velvet: algorithms for de novo short read assembly using de Bruijn graphs. Genome Res. 18, 821–829 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3515">76</a></sup> and then Oases 0.1.18<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Schulz, M.H., Zerbino, D.R., Vingron, M. &amp; Birney, E. Oases: robust de novo RNA-seq assembly across the dynamic range of expression levels. Bioinformatics 28, 1086–1092 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3519">77</a></sup>. Several values of hash length were tested to optimize the assembly: 39, 51, 63, 65, 69 and 73. The obtained assemblies were compared for their ability to map raw reads using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589–595 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3523">78</a></sup>. We consequently decided for a hash length of 73. The transcript assembly was then searched for redundancy. Contigs sharing identity over ≥95% of the length of the shortest sequence in a set of putative homologous sequences were clustered. The final transcript assembly contained 50,313 contigs, with a total of 36,479,993 nucleotides. Three transcriptomes (Zeng <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Zeng, Y., Conner, J. &amp; Ozias-Akins, P. Identification of ovule transcripts from the Apospory-Specific Genomic Region (ASGR)-carrier chromosome. BMC Genomics 12, 206 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3531">16</a></sup>, Rajaram <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Rajaram, V. et al. Pearl millet [Pennisetum glaucum (L.) R. Br.] consensus linkage map constructed using four RIL mapping populations and newly developed EST-SSRs. BMC Genomics 14, 159 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3538">17</a></sup>, and the transcriptome data generated at IRD, France, available under BioProject ID <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA391885">PRJNA391885</a>) were combined and clustered using CDHIT-EST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Fu, L., Niu, B., Zhu, Z., Wu, S. &amp; Li, W. CD-HIT: accelerated for clustering the next-generation sequencing data. Bioinformatics 28, 3150–3152 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3549">79</a></sup> with default parameters to eliminate redundancy at the sequence level. Then, CAP3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Huang, X. &amp; Madan, A. CAP3: A DNA sequence assembly program. Genome Res. 9, 868–877 (1999)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3553">80</a></sup> was used to assemble the contigs. Ns on either end of the resultant contigs were trimmed. Finally, contigs of at least 200 bp in length were used in gene annotation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Gene family and phylogenetic analysis.</h3><p>For gene family analysis, BLASTP with an E-value cutoff of ≤ 1.0e-05 was used to compare all annotated pearl millet protein sequences against a protein data set of 10 sequenced plant species (<i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3567">20</a></sup>, <i>Brachypodium</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3573">21</a></sup>, banana<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="D'Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3577">22</a></sup>, barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Mayer, K.F. et al. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711–716 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3581">23</a></sup>, foxtail millet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Bennetzen, J.L. et al. Reference genome sequence of the model plant Setaria. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 555–561 (2012)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3586">11</a></sup>, maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Schnable, P.S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3590">24</a></sup>, rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yu, J. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science 296, 79–92 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3594">15</a></sup>, sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3598">14</a></sup>, soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3602">25</a></sup> and <i>T. urartu</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ling, H.Q. et al. Draft genome of the wheat A-genome progenitor Triticum urartu. Nature 496, 87–90 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3609">26</a></sup>). The proteins were clustered using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3613">27</a></sup> (–mode 3) to define gene families which included both paralogs and orthologs. The number of gene families in each species and genus was calculated based on the composition of the OrthoMCL clusters. Genes that were single copy in an OrthoMCL cluster for all species analyzed were selected to construct a phylogenetic tree using the PhyML (parameters: -d nt -b -4 -m HKY85 -a e -c 4 -t e) program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307–321 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3617">81</a></sup> (Version 3.0). Divergence times between pearl millet and other species were estimated using MCMCTREE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3621">82</a></sup> with default parameter. First, the gene family size for each species was calculated based on the output of OrthoMCL, and rooted tree in newick format. CAFE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J.P. &amp; Hahn, M.W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269–1271 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3625">83</a></sup> (-p 0.05 -t 4 -r 10000 -filter) was used to predict the expansion and contraction of gene family numbers based on the phylogenetic tree and gene family statistics.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Population analysis.</h3><p>Population genetic analyses of the PMiGAP lines, including PCA and diversity detection were conducted essentially as described for rice by Xu and colleagues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Xu, X. et al. Resequencing 50 accessions of cultivated and wild rice yields markers for identifying agronomically important genes. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 105–111 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3638">84</a></sup>. We used a subset of 450,000 SNPs, with a missing rate &lt;10% across PMiGAP lines and wild accessions. Briefly, for PCA, eigenvector decomposition of the SNP genotype data was calculated using the R function eigen<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Patterson, N., Price, A.L. &amp; Reich, D. Population structure and eigenanalysis. PLoS Genet. 2, e190 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3642">85</a></sup>. A Tracey-Wisdom test with default parameter settings was performed to determine the significance of axes using the twstats program. To build a phylogenetic tree, the percentage of pairwise nucleotide differences between individuals (p-distance) was calculated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Patterson, N., Price, A.L. &amp; Reich, D. Population structure and eigenanalysis. PLoS Genet. 2, e190 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3646">85</a></sup>. The program fneighbor (PHYLIPNEW v3.69.650 within the package EMBOSS v6.6.0.0; parameter: -matrixtype s -treetype n) was used to construct a neighbor joining tree. The resulting tree was edited and visualized using MEGA5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731–2739 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3650">86</a></sup> by choosing Radiation style. Population structure was assessed using the program Snmf (–k K –c)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Frichot, E., Mathieu, F., Trouillon, T., Bouchard, G. &amp; François, O. Fast and efficient estimation of individual ancestry coefficients. Genetics 196, 973–983 (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3654">87</a></sup>. Five runs were performed and the values with the smallest Cross-Entropy for K from 2 to 7 were selected to generate the structure graphs. To better assess the structure, we performed the analysis in a geographical context, using TESS3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Caye, K., Deist, T.M., Martins, H., Michel, O. &amp; François, O. TESS3: fast inference of spatial population structure and genome scans for selection. Mol. Ecol. Resour. 16, 540–548 (2016)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3659">88</a></sup> that takes geographical coordinates of the sample into account. Furthermore, parameters of population genetic diversity π, θ<sub>ω</sub> and differentiation (F<sub>ST</sub>) were calculated based on the SNP data as described earlier<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Patterson, N., Price, A.L. &amp; Reich, D. Population structure and eigenanalysis. PLoS Genet. 2, e190 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3667">85</a></sup>. To analyze diversity across the genome, we used a window of 100 kb and calculated the diversity π, θ<sub>ω</sub> and differentiation F<sub>ST</sub> for each window for PMiGAP lines and wild accessions using BioPerl modules (Bio::PopGen::Statistics and Bio::PopGen::PopStats) on a sliding window of 100 kb using genotype data. The effective sequence length (without Ns) in each window was used as the denominator to calculate per-bp values. We then calculated a minus log of the ratio of diversity between cultivated and wild samples: –log (π cultivated/ π wild). For this log ratio of diversity and differentiation, we retained the most extreme values using a classical threshold of 95% for a unilateral test and a more stringent threshold of 99.5%. This later stronger stringent threshold was used to identify the most likely gene candidates selected during domestication. Loci with higher levels of differentiation (most extreme F<sub>ST</sub>) and stronger loss of diversity in the cultivated compared to the wild accessions were considered to be provisionally involved in the domestication process.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Identification of NBS domain, TIR domain, LRR motif and CC motif.</h3><p>All pearl millet proteins were assessed for the presence of NBS domains (PF00931, NB-ARC) using the Hidden Markov Model based method implemented in hmmsearch (version 3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Eddy, S.R. Profile hidden Markov models. Bioinformatics 14, 755–763 (1998)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3686">89</a></sup> with an e-value cutoff = 1. To filter false positive hits, all identified NBS containing proteins were screened against the Pfam-A database. NBS domains that overlapped with other domains identified at lower e-values were filtered out. Likewise, the TIR domain (PF01582) was used as query against all pearl millet proteins with hmmsearch and further checked by looking at the overlapping domains. To detect LRR motifs, predicted NBS encoding proteins were searched against 10 LRR families in LRR clan (CL0022) with an e-value cutoff = 1. All regions predicted as LRR motifs and not overlapping with other domains identified with lower e-values were considered real LRR motifs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">SNP calling, structural variation and linkage disequilibrium (LD) decay.</h3><p>Sequence reads generated for the B- and R- lines, PMiGAP lines, and parental lines and wild lines were mapped separately to the pearl millet genome assembly using BWA (v0.6) (parameter: aln -n 0.04 -o 1 -e 30 -i 15 -d 10 -l 35 -k 2 -m 2000000 -t 4 -M 3 -O 11 -E 4 -R 30 -q 0 -I; sampe -a 500 -o 100000 -n 3 -N 10 -c 1.0e-05). The BAM files generated by BWA were sorted and provided as input to the GATK software package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3698">90</a></sup> (Version 3.1-1). The UnifiedGenotyper module within GATK was used to detect SNP variants. The variants were filtered using VariantFiltration, a module from GATK (parameters: QD &lt; 2.0 || FS &gt; 60.0 || MQ &lt; 40.0 || HaplotypeScore &gt; 13.0; parameters for indel: QD &lt; 2.0 || FS &gt; 200.0), and the number of variants distribution in intergenic/coding regions were calculated. The data used in the downstream analysis were controlled with MAF 0.05 and missing rate 0.5. SNPs with a mean depth &gt; 100 and missing rate &gt; 0.5 were removed. The remaining SNPs were used in further analyses. Variants for wild lines that used in population structure and domestication analysis were detected together with PMiGAP accessions and processed with the same strategy (BAM and VCF files available at <a href="http://ceg.icrisat.org/ipmgsc/">http://ceg.icrisat.org/ipmgsc/</a>).</p><p>The BAM files from each resequenced accession was analyzed by Breakdancer (version 1.1.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Chen, K. et al. BreakDancer: an algorithm for high-resolution mapping of genomic structural variation. Nat. Methods 6, 677–681 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3712">91</a></sup> with default parameters to detect structural variation namely, deletions, insertions, inversions, and intra-chromosomal translocations. Breakdancer results of accessions that come from a same line (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM49">Supplementary Table 32</a>) were combined to remove redundancy and to calculate the number and length of the rearrangements.</p><p>Using SNP data sets from PMiGAP lines, Haploview software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Barrett, J.C., Fry, B., Maller, J. &amp; Daly, M.J. Haploview: analysis and visualization of LD and haplotype maps. Bioinformatics 21, 263–265 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3722">92</a></sup> (-maxdistance 250 –minMAF 0.05 -dprime -memory 5096) was used to calculate correlation coefficient (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup>) values for LD. The average (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup>) values between pairwise distances (bp) were calculated and figures were plotted using R.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Statistical analysis.</h3><p><i>Phenotyping data and GWAS analysis.</i> For establishing marker trait associations, 288 test cross hybrids were generated by crossing of PMiGAP lines as pollen parents with a common seed parent ICMA 843-22. These hybrids were grouped by maturity (early, medium early, medium and late) and phenotyped for 20 morphological traits under two drought stress conditions (early and late stress) along with controls (or no stress) for two years (2011, 2012). Experiments were conducted in an alpha-lattice designs with two replications in three test environments during Summer 2011 and 2012 (January to May) in the red precision (RP) experimental fields at the ICRISAT, Patancheru, Telengana, India (545 m above mean sea level, 17.53° N latitude and 78.27° E longitude). The early maturity group consisted of lines which had days to 50% flowering (DFF) from 42-52 days; the medium-early maturity group consisted of lines with DFF from 53-57 days; the medium maturity group consisted of entries with DFF from 58-62 days; the late maturity group consisted of lines which recorded more than 62 days for DFF. Early drought stress is a more severe stress imposed by withholding irrigation from about one week before flowering until maturity. Late stress is a less severe drought stress initiated during early grain-filling by withholding irrigation from 50% flowering time till maturity.</p><p>The three test environments consisted of early-onset of stress, late-onset stress, and a common, fully-irrigated non-stress treatment. Drought stress was imposed by withholding irrigation from about one week before flowering in early-onset treatment, while drought stress in the late-onset treatment was imposed by withholding irrigation from 50% flowering. Data were recorded for a total of 20 traits namely, grain yield (GYHA), panicle yield (HYHA), panicle harvest index (PHI), time to 75% flowering (TB), plant height (PH), panicle length (EL), panicle diameter (ED), panicle number (HCHA), number of tillers per plant (Till), biomass yield (BM), grain harvest index (HI), thousand grain weight (TGW), grain number per panicle (GNP), grain number per m<sup>2</sup> (GNM2), agronomic score (AgS), stover dry matter fraction (DMF) and vegetative growth index (GI). PH, EL, and ED were measured on the main stems of five representative plants of each entry in a plot at maturity. At harvest, data were recorded from the harvested area on plant population (PCHA), panicle numbers (HCHA) and fresh stover yield (FSWTHA). Effective tiller number (Till) was calculated as the ratio HCHA/PCHA. HYHA, GYHA and TGW were recorded after oven drying for about 24 h. Stover dry matter yield (DMY) was estimated from plot FSWTHA using the fresh and dry weights of a chopped subsample of stover from each plot. BM was calculated as HYHA + DMY on a plot basis. Grain number per panicle (GNP) was derived from primary data as [(GYHA/HCHA)/ (TGW/1000)]. Grain harvest index was calculated as the ratio between grain yield and biomass yield at harvest, and panicle harvest index as the ratio between grain weight and panicle weight. Flowering time was recorded as days from seedling emergence to stigma emergence for 75% of the main shoots in a plot. The traits measured include grain yield (kg/ha), panicle yield (kg/ha), panicle harvest index (%), time to 50% flowering (number of days), plant height (cm), panicle length (cm), panicle diameter (cm), panicle number, tillers per plant, biomass yield (kg/ha), vegetative growth index (kg/ha/day), grain harvest index (%), fresh stover yield (t/ha), stover dry matter yield (kg/ha), stover dry matter fraction, 1000-grain mass (g), grain number per panicle, and grain number per m<sup>2</sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM73">Supplementary Data set 2</a>). Analysis of variance for all traits was performed using the PROC MIXED procedure in SAS 9.3 (SAS Institute Inc 2013) with Kenward-Roger degree of freedom approximation method considering replicates and accessions as fixed effects, whereas incomplete blocks within each replication were considered as random effects for combined intra and inter block analysis. Best linear unbiased estimates (BLUEs) were calculated for all accessions.</p><p>For GWAS analysis, a total of 3,117,056 SNPs retained after filtering the minor alleles (MAF&lt;0.05) and 20% missing data were used. Marker-trait associations were established using AOV model with a bloc effect for maturity group in R (Phenotype<span class="stix">∼</span>Bloc+SNP). We tested the suitability of the model by plotting the observed P-values from the association test against an expected (cumulative) probability distribution. These quantile-quantile (q-q) plots clearly indicated that we corrected properly for population stratification (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM47">Supplementary Fig. 23</a>). Significance of associations between loci and traits were determined adjusting for multiple testing by using FDR at a 0.001 threshold level and considering p value lower than 10<sup>−10</sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">
                  <i>Genomic prediction analysis for testcross performance.</i>
                </h3><p>Grain yield performance of 259 PMiGAP lines was used for hybrid prediction analysis. In our analysis, flowering time was considered as a cofactor. For genomic prediction analysis, we performed a one-stage phenotypic data analysis on 259 PMiGAP lines as test cross hybrid trials using a linear mixed model that included genotype, flowering time, year, stress, interaction among genotype, stress and year, replication, incomplete block and residual effects. The effect of flowering time was always assumed to be fixed. When estimating variance components, all other effects were assumed to be random. To get the BLUE of each line, we set the genotype effect as fixed.</p><p>The heritability on the line mean basis was estimated as <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw257/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq1_HTML.gif" alt="">where <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw167/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq2_HTML.gif" style="width:167px;max-width:none;" alt=""> and <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw16/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq3_HTML.gif" style="width:16px;max-width:none;" alt=""> are variance components arising from genotype, genotype × year interaction, genotype × stress interaction, the three-way interaction and the residual, respectively. <i>y</i>, <i>s</i> and <i>r</i> are the number of different years, stresses, and replications. In addition, we calculated the BLUE for each genotype in each environment (stress versus control) across years. That is, for each environment we fitted a linear mixed model including genotype, flowering time, year, genotype × year interaction, replication, incomplete block and residual effects. The assumptions of the parameters were similar to above. The heritability in this case was estimated as <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw147/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq4_HTML.gif" style="width:147px;max-width:none;" alt="">All phenotypic data analyses were done using the ASreml- R 3 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Butler, D.G., Cullis, B.R., Gilmour, A.R. &amp; Gogel, B.J. ASReml-R Reference Manual. Technical report, Queensland Department of Primary Industries. 
                    http://www.vsni.co.uk/software/asreml/
                    
                   (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d28003e3819">93</a></sup>.</p><p>A total of 2,235,060 SNPs with &lt;20% missing rates were used with above mentioned phenotyping data for genomic prediction analysis. We used the genomic best linear unbiased prediction (G-BLUP) model for genomic selection: <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw103/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq5_HTML.gif" style="width:103px;max-width:none;" alt="">, where <i>y</i> refers to <i>n</i>-dimensional vector of phenotypic records, 1<sub><i>n</i></sub> is an <i>n</i>-dimensional vector of ones, <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw9/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq6_HTML.gif" style="width:9px;max-width:none;" alt=""> is the mean, <i>g</i> is an <i>n</i>-dimensional vector of additive genotypic values and <i>e</i> is an <i>n</i>-dimensional vector of residual terms.</p><p>In the model <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw105/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq7_HTML.gif" style="width:105px;max-width:none;" alt=""> we assume that <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw9/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq8_HTML.gif" style="width:9px;max-width:none;" alt=""> is a fixed parameter, and <i>g</i>, <i>e</i> are random parameters with <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw59/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq9_HTML.gif" style="width:59px;max-width:none;" alt=""> and <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw78/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq10_HTML.gif" style="width:78px;max-width:none;" alt="">, where <i>G</i> denotes the <i>n</i> × <i>n</i> genomic relationship matrix. <i>G</i> was calculated as follows: Let <i>X</i> = (<i>x</i><sub><i>ij</i></sub>) be the <i>n</i> × <i>p</i> matrix of SNP markers, where <i>x</i><sub><i>ij</i></sub> equals the number of a chosen allele at the <i>j</i><sup>th</sup> locus for the <i>i</i><sup>th</sup> genotype. Let <i>p</i><sub><i>j</i></sub> be the allele frequency of the <i>j</i><sup>th</sup> marker. <i>W</i> = (<i>w</i><sub><i>ij</i></sub>) is an <i>n</i> × <i>p</i> matrix with <i>w</i><sub><i>ij</i></sub> = <i>x</i><sub><i>ij</i></sub> − 2<i>p</i><sub><i>j</i></sub>.</p><p>Then we have <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw127/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq11_HTML.gif" style="width:127px;max-width:none;" alt="">Note that when calculating the kinship coefficient for two genotypes, only those markers without missing values in both genotypes were considered.</p><p>The accuracy of genomic prediction was evaluated by fivefold cross-validation with a total of 100 cross-validation runs. The cross-validated prediction accuracy was calculated as the Pearson product-moment correlation between predicted and observed genotypic values of the lines in the test set. The GBLUP model was implemented using the R software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="R Core Team. R: A language and environment for statistical computing. R Foundation for Statistical Computing, Vienna, Austria. 
                    http://www.R-project.org/
                    
                   (2014)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d28003e4013">94</a></sup>.</p><p>
                  <i>Hybrid prediction analysis.</i>
                </p><p>Grain yield of 64 pearl millet hybrids grown at five locations in India (Jamnagar, Anand, SK Nagar, Mahuva, Kothara) during the time period 2004-2013 was measured. Trials were conducted during 2004, 2005, 2006, 2008, 2011 and 2012 in Kharif, Summer and pre rabi season. However, during 2007, 2009, 2010 and 2013 trials were conducted in only Kharif and Summer. We adopted randomized block design with a spacing of 60 cm between the rows and 10-15 cm between the plants and adopted standard agronomic practices. The 64 hybrids were generated by crossing 20 male and 23 female lines.</p><p>By using the grain yield phenotyping data for 64 hybrids as mentioned above, we used the following linear mixed model to estimate the variance components as well as BLUEs:</p><p>Yield <span class="stix">∼</span>Genotype + Replication.</p><p>To estimate variance components, all effects were treated as random. The BLUEs for each environment were calculated by the same mixed model but modelling genotype as fixed effect. Repeatability was estimated as <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw141/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq12_HTML.gif" style="width:141px;max-width:none;" alt="">, where N<sub>R</sub> refers number of replications, <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw17/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq13_HTML.gif" style="width:17px;max-width:none;" alt=""> refers to genetic variance, and <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw15/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq14_HTML.gif" style="width:15px;max-width:none;" alt=""> refers to residual variance. Four environments with repeatability lower than 0.5 were removed from further analysis. The BLUEs of the 64 hybrids of each environment were used for an analysis across environments by fitting following model:</p><p>Yield<span class="stix">∼</span>Genotype + Environment.</p><p>The genotype effects were treated as fixed and the environment effects as random. The distribution of the BLUEs across environments approximated a normal distribution. The variance components of genotypes <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw17/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq15_HTML.gif" style="width:17px;max-width:none;" alt="">, genotype x environment interactions <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw38/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq16_HTML.gif" style="width:38px;max-width:none;" alt=""> and of the residuals <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw16/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq17_HTML.gif" style="width:16px;max-width:none;" alt=""> were estimated using a one-step model. Broad-sense heritability was then calculated as the ratio of genotypic to phenotypic variance:</p>
                  <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw144/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_IEq18_HTML.gif" style="width:144px;max-width:none;" alt="">
                <p>where <i>l</i> refers to the number of environments and r is the average number of replications per environment. The hybrid prediction was based on 302,110 high-quality SNP markers obtained from 580 B- and R- lines. We used ridge regression-BLUP considering additive and dominance effects to predict the hybrid performance. Details of the implementation of the models have been described earlier<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Zhao, Y., Zeng, J., Fernando, R. &amp; Reif, J.C. Genomic prediction of hybrid wheat performance. Crop Sci. 53, 802–810 (2013)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d28003e4109">95</a></sup>. Briefly, the general form of the model is defined as the following:</p><div id="Equ1" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><img src="//media.springernature.com/lw156/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.3943/MediaObjects/41587_2017_Article_BFnbt3943_Equ1_HTML.gif" class="u-display-block" alt=""></div></div><p>where <i>1</i><sub><i>n</i></sub> is a vector of ones and <i>n</i> is the number of hybrids, μ refers to the overall mean across all four locations. <i>Z</i><sub><i>A</i></sub> and <i>Z</i><sub><i>D</i></sub> are <i>n</i> × <i>m</i> design matrices for the additive and dominance effects of the markers, where <i>m</i> refers to the number of markers. The elements of <i>Z</i><sub><i>A</i></sub> are -1, 0, 1, and elements of <i>Z</i><sub><i>D</i></sub> is 0, 1. While <i>a</i> = (<i>a</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>a</i><sub>2</sub>, ..., <i>a</i><sub><i>m</i></sub>)<sup><i>T</i></sup> and <i>d</i> = (<i>d</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>d</i><sub>2</sub>, ..., <i>d</i><sub><i>m</i></sub>)<sup><i>T</i></sup> are the vectors of length m, and <i>a</i><sub><i>i</i></sub> <i>d</i><sub><i>i</i></sub> denote the additive and dominance effects for the <i>i</i><sup>th</sup> marker, respectively. <i>e</i> = (<i>e</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>e</i><sub>2</sub>, ..., <i>e</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>)<sup><i>T</i></sup> is a vector of length <i>n</i> and <i>e</i><sub><i>j</i></sub> is the residual for the j<sup>th</sup> hybrid.</p><p>Prediction accuracy was studied using cross validations. In each cross validation, 48 hybrids were randomly selected as training set and the remaining 16 hybrids were used as test set. The cross validation was run 500 times and accuracy was estimated as the Pearson correlation coefficient between predicted and observed values standardized with the square root of the heritability (h = 0.76). Next, we used all 64 hybrids as a training set and predicted the hybrid performance of 167,910 possible single-cross combinations among the 580 inbred lines (260 B-lines and 320 R-lines). Based on the predicted values, we selected 0.1% hybrids that had the highest predicted yields (170/167,910 hybrids). Of those 170 hybrids, 11 have been bred so far and are thus a subset of the 64 phenotyped hybrids. The remaining 159 hybrids are based on parental inbred lines that have never been used for hybrid breeding and could be tested in the field. All analyses were done using the ASreml-R 3 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Butler, D.G., Cullis, B.R., Gilmour, A.R. &amp; Gogel, B.J. ASReml-R Reference Manual. Technical report, Queensland Department of Primary Industries. 
                    http://www.vsni.co.uk/software/asreml/
                    
                   (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.3943#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d28003e4265">93</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Data availability.</h3><p>Genome sequence assembly and annotation data: BioProject ID <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA294988">PRJNA294988</a>; BioSample ID <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/biosample/?term=SAMN04124419">SAMN04124419</a>. Resequencing data: SRA <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRP063925">SRP063925</a>. Transcriptome data: BioProject ID <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA391885">PRJNA391885</a>. BAM and SNP files are available at <a href="http://ceg.icrisat.org/ipmgsc">http://ceg.icrisat.org/ipmgsc</a>. GigaScience Database record: <a href="https://doi.org/10.5524/100192">http://dx.doi.org/10.5524/100192</a> Scripts used in the MS are available at <a href="https://github.com/ICRISAT-CEG/PM-Scripts.git">https://github.com/ICRISAT-CEG/PM-Scripts.git</a></p><p>A <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.3943#MOESM48">Life Sciences Reporting Summary</a> is available.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec28" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec28-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec28">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec28-content"><p><b>Publisher's note:</b> Springer Nature remains neutral with regard to jurisdictional claims in published maps and institutional affiliations.</p></div></div></section>
